<div id="review_64339327" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64339327" />
    <a title="Nancy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1882126-nancy"><img alt="Nancy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595275409p2/1882126.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64339327?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy" href="/user/show/1882126-nancy">Nancy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1882126-nancy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64339327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer343405188989217574">Spoilers possible... The plot of this book is about a modern graduate student's research into a primary source concurrent with the Salem witch trials. There were parts that were extremely well written. The historical sequences, particularly the hangings, were eloquent at times. However, I found so much = the storyline, events, locales and characters predictable, and at the same time, somewhat unbelieveable. I was distracted by the timeline, which seemed unrealistic. How could the main character </span>
  <span id="freeText343405188989217574" style="display:none">Spoilers possible... The plot of this book is about a modern graduate student's research into a primary source concurrent with the Salem witch trials. There were parts that were extremely well written. The historical sequences, particularly the hangings, were eloquent at times. However, I found so much = the storyline, events, locales and characters predictable, and at the same time, somewhat unbelieveable. I was distracted by the timeline, which seemed unrealistic. How could the main character clean out this old family property, do all that primary source sleuthing, and have the paper written by the end of September? I felt as the clues presented themselves, Connie jumped to lucky conclusions, made assumptions and inferences. There were aspects that I figured out before the characters did, and I'm not a colonial history buff. Among other things, I guessed what all the bottles in the kitchen were. I knew the significance of the Puritan names and foresaw the connect between Deliverance and "Constance". I knew receipt and recipe were inter-changeable. An almanac was no earthshattering surprise. I just couldn't buy Connie or her instructor as Harvard material. <br /><br />That being said, it was a fun book in an escapist sort of way. A quick read, but I was expecting more. Good, entertaining but not serious or profound. </span>
  <a data-text-id="343405188989217574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1594016547" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1594016547" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1594016547?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Khaemba" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Khaemba" href="/user/show/41380936-bill-khaemba">Bill Khaemba</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41380936-bill-khaemba?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41380936-bill-khaemba?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41380936-bill-khaemba?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1594016547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6388415065513572217"><b> “But remember. Just because you don't believe in something doesn't mean it isn't real.” </b><br/><br/><i> First of all fun fact about me, I love witch related stories like a lot </i><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1463778611ra/19158652.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/> I grew up watching <b> Charmed and Sabrina the teenage witch </b>, they were my life. So going into this book was solely based on the fact that it’s about witches and the history behind the famous <b> *Salem Witch trials* </b>. I have to say that this book receives a lot of bad reviews on goodreads but I enjoyed it, the writing-style was excellent a</span>
  <span id="freeText6388415065513572217" style="display:none"><b> “But remember. Just because you don't believe in something doesn't mean it isn't real.” </b><br><br><i> First of all fun fact about me, I love witch related stories like a lot </i><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1463778611ra/19158652.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br> I grew up watching <b> Charmed and Sabrina the teenage witch </b>, they were my life. So going into this book was solely based on the fact that it’s about witches and the history behind the famous <b> *Salem Witch trials* </b>. I have to say that this book receives a lot of bad reviews on goodreads but I enjoyed it, the writing-style was excellent and the history part was just juicy and tantalizing. Her amazing attention to detail was my favorite part, like the main character would describe the feel of old books and settings of a library which just enhanced my excitement.<br><br> <b> <blockquote> "Connie and Liz had often joked that grad students make terrible dinner party guest, because they cannot be gotten away from reading the spine of the books..." </blockquote>
<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1398159287ra/9377709.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> </b><br><br>The book essentially follows our main character <b> *Connie* </b> who is a smart history-buff who gets sent by her mom during the summer to clean up and sell her grandmother’s house but when she arrives at the abandoned house, she stumbles upon a key in a bible that has a scroll attached to it written “Deliverance Dane” and being the history geek that she is, her journey to discover a missing part in the Salem Witch Trials in 1680s begins.<br><br>This book was told in Dual time-frame, In the 1990s and Interludes of the 1680s, which I absolutely love because at some point the similarities between them start showing up and I live for that stuff. I would advise you to go into this book not expecting a fast pace plot driven book, it’s definitely on the slower side and that was kind of a problem but the story still held my interest. The author also managed to add some witchcraft, magic and spells which just made the book special and also how she showcased the origin of the pointy-hat, broomstick riding, cat-owning witch cliché was so cool. In retrospect you could tell the author did her research and her passion on the subject shone through the main character.<br><br>Overall the book surprised me at page 150 I was sure it would be an average book but It added magical realism and referenced “THE BOOK OF SHADOWS” *Charmed fans will get it*, I was sold. Highly recommend it if you like historical fiction, a hint of magic and old books. <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1463778611ra/19158653.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Thanks for reading :)<br><br><b> Follow Blog: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://kenyanlibrary.wordpress.com/">https://kenyanlibrary.wordpress.com/</a> </b></span>

<div id="review_2883222785" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2883222785" />
    <a title="Jaksen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7233842-jaksen"><img alt="Jaksen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455934399p2/7233842.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2883222785?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaksen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaksen" href="/user/show/7233842-jaksen">Jaksen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2883222785" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5484147227174062076">Interesting book, and as a New Englander it felt very real - the setting, the language, the little nuances concerning customs and a way not only of life, but of 'thinking.' My family goes way back to one of the main events in this book: the Salem Witch Trials. My earliest American ancestors lived in Ipswich and the family patriarch actually signed a petition - along with his four adult sons - asking for leniency for the accused witches. Consider that. Consider the repercussions which might have </span>
  <span id="freeText5484147227174062076" style="display:none">Interesting book, and as a New Englander it felt very real - the setting, the language, the little nuances concerning customs and a way not only of life, but of 'thinking.' My family goes way back to one of the main events in this book: the Salem Witch Trials. My earliest American ancestors lived in Ipswich and the family patriarch actually signed a petition - along with his four adult sons - asking for leniency for the accused witches. Consider that. Consider the repercussions which might have followed. <br /><br />Okay enough about me. Here's the story: Constance Goodwin, Phd candidate, is looking for a topic for her final thesis. Her mentor/advisor is an old curmudgeon of a man, but happily enthusiastic when Constance (conveniently) is asked to sort out an old property which belonged to her late grandmother. Clean up an old house, get it sold, pay off the taxes which are long-overdue, etc. Yep, very happy he is when Constance - Connie - finds clues in the house which will eventually lead her to researching Deliverance Dane, a maybe-witch, who might have been part of the Salem Witch trials in 1692.<br /><br />This is a book which I could see going only one way - and I was right. The interesting thing is, I had no idea how it was going to get there. Ever have that happen with a book? It's like an 'ahah!' moment, or a 'I bet I know what's going to happen!' and you get it right, or mostly so, but the paths to get there...<br /><br />There are so many of them! And in that way I was pleasantly surprised by the myriad twists and turns the story took as Connie researches and talks to people - all done realistically, btw - in old libraries, town halls and town and church archives. That part was totally fascinating.<br /><br />There's a romantic element, too, but it's not overdone; it fits the story perfectly. There's also a few moments of: didn't see that coming, and even one event where I was a bit underwhelmed and - is it a word? - undersurprised.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><br />Connie finds an old Bible in her grandmother's house and I thought yay! Bibles throughout history are often great repositories of family information: who was born when and where; who got married to whom; who died etc. One reason for this: Paper was precious but most Bibles had a few blank pages in the front or back where a family could write things - important things! And two, almost every family from the 1600's on had a Bible in the house. So when Connie finds only one thing in this old, 1600's era Bible, a little key with a slip of paper in it, I was left thinking: What kind of New England family doesn't write stuff in their Bible? Okay, but this does fit the story near the end so I got over it. Kind of.<br /><br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />As Connie does her research, interacts with her crabby old advisor, falls in love and attempts to deal with an old house which has no phone or electricity, the story moves swiftly along. The writing is elegant, but def. not overdone. Ms Howe is one of those writers who I would read just to read her descriptions. The story is mainly a contemporary one, but there are a few chapters set in Salem of 1692 which only add to the overall atmosphere. A very satisfying story over all.<br /><br />I look forward to reading Ms. Howe's newest book.<br /><br />Four stars. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5484147227174062076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_156499203" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156499203" />
    <a title="Mindy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1040018-mindy"><img alt="Mindy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425337263p2/1040018.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156499203?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mindy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mindy" href="/user/show/1040018-mindy">Mindy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156499203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1452886598857465675">SO boring!!! If this book hadn't been recommended to me there's no way on earth I would have kept going. Until the last quarter of the book I could've put it down at any time and never given it a second thought. I thought much of the imagery was over the top and some of it just plain stupid. For example, saying a man in court looks like he smelled of burnt leaves. What is that? Books should paint a picture. Not only do I still not see the picture, it distracted me from the book. Or calling the c</span>
  <span id="freeText1452886598857465675" style="display:none">SO boring!!! If this book hadn't been recommended to me there's no way on earth I would have kept going. Until the last quarter of the book I could've put it down at any time and never given it a second thought. I thought much of the imagery was over the top and some of it just plain stupid. For example, saying a man in court looks like he smelled of burnt leaves. What is that? Books should paint a picture. Not only do I still not see the picture, it distracted me from the book. Or calling the color puce cheerful. Really? Or describing the names Deliverance, Mercy and Prudence as intense. How so? They don't seem particulary intense plus they were very popular in the that time period (which a historian of that time period would know so they should be pretty commonplace to her, not intense). I also didn't find the cluelessness of the main character believeable. She's not just an intelligent, educated woman (I understand there are some very smart people that have very little common sense), she's a very good researcher which, to me means that number one, she pays attention to details, and number two, that she has a wide knowledge base. What was going on was so incredibly obvious from the very beginning of the book that it was really annoying that she was so stupid about it. It felt like the author was trying to lead you up to a big surprise but there were no surprises. I could've told you the whole plot from very early on in the book. And then after all the mind-numbingly boring build-up, the climax was a huge let down. Really, that's it? And the stupid little ending with the old couple on the bench...there aren't words. I honestly can't think of any redeeming qualities of this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1452886598857465675" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer741603745565126680">This book felt like it was intended for middle-schoolers; pedestrian writing, uninteresting characters, and a connect the dots plot.   <span id="freeText741603745565126680" style="display:none">This book felt like it was intended for middle-schoolers; pedestrian writing, uninteresting characters, and a connect the dots plot. The historical sections sounded like they came straight from Wikipedia. <br /><br />Connie is contradictory and rather dim. One minute she barely remembers her grandmother, the next she vividly recalls a holiday with her. I could have overlooked that, if the story had been more convincing.<br /><br />I am beyond baffled by Connie's inexperience with the library at the college she attends. As a Ph.D candidate in colonial history, she would have spent countless hours in the library. She should have known that the digital catalog only went back to 1972, and that anything older needed to be researched in the card catalog. Her unfamiliarity with colonial language, names, and commonplace spelling variants was perplexing as well.<br /><br />There were several scientific and historical errors, and I found myself exclaiming in disbelief at the time, but the specifics elude me now. I only continued reading in hopes that the ending would be interesting. It wasn't.<br /><br />If you liked (or wanted to like) this book, you might also enjoy:<br /><br />✱ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2861147.The_Minister_s_Daughter" title="The Minister's Daughter by Julie Hearn" rel="nofollow noopener">The Minister's Daughter</a><br />✱ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8667848.A_Discovery_of_Witches__All_Souls_Trilogy___1_" title="A Discovery of Witches (All Souls Trilogy, #1) by Deborah Harkness" rel="nofollow noopener">A Discovery of Witches</a></span>

<div id="review_107937743" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/107937743" />
    <a title="Melissa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3319099-melissa"><img alt="Melissa" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/107937743?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa" href="/user/show/3319099-melissa">Melissa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer107937743" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5381810740682688614">I'll admit, I picked this book up in the New Fiction section of my library based on the title and the cover (great design). Based on the description and the excited blurbs (by author's I've never heard of, I'll admit) I checked it out hoping it might be something like A.S.Byatt's very literate, very suspenseful <i>Posession</i>. Boy was I mislead. It reads more like a weirdly misguided YA novel (misguided in the thought that the average YA reader would be interested in the academia). The writer really </span>
  <span id="freeText5381810740682688614" style="display:none">I'll admit, I picked this book up in the New Fiction section of my library based on the title and the cover (great design). Based on the description and the excited blurbs (by author's I've never heard of, I'll admit) I checked it out hoping it might be something like A.S.Byatt's very literate, very suspenseful <i>Posession</i>. Boy was I mislead. It reads more like a weirdly misguided YA novel (misguided in the thought that the average YA reader would be interested in the academia). The writer really doesn't trust her audience to have any kind of imagination or background knowledge.<br /><br />I couldn't get past Chapter 4 of this. There were too many false notes. For example, would a young Ivy League grad student fully immersed in researching Colonial American history really be aware of the Seattle grunge music scene in the early summer of 1991? "Nevermind" wasn't released until September of '91. Of course, that could be nitpicking, but there are other inconsistencies. Isn't it simply enough to point out a character speaks with an old fashioned Brahmin Bostonian accent? Why would a native of Concord, Ma even notice such an accent? Yet there is an overly lengthy passage describing it from her point of view. The main character has spent four years at Mount Holyoke and two years under this man's tutelage at Harvard yet she finds herself distracted by his Bostonian accent? While I'm on the subject of accents, the author seemed to be obsessed with them. She has some characters speak in jarring Massachusetts dialect. I am really not a fan of indicating dialect with clumsy phonetic spelling. I think it is a poor substitute for thoughtful writing and it is condescending to the reader. I was born and raised in Southeastern Mass and Rhode Island. I <i>know</i> the accent. I don't need it spelled out for me. Of course, she is not writing for an audience of one but I think most Americans these days can imagine a Massachusetts accent. Certainly anyone who has heard a Kennedy speak (and these days, who <i>hasn't</i>)is able to conjure a facsimile. I realize that not all Massachusetts accents are the same but when she has certain working-class characters speak in dialect it is with an air of condescension I find grating.<br /><br />I would have let these things go had it not been for the silly, Lifetime Original Movie tone in which the plot is slowly unveiled. The helpful, loving friend who accompanies her to the mysterious old house which,for some reason, although it is located in Marblehead, an area of Massachusetts which is home to a large community of artists, has not been snapped up and renovated. And by the way, does this friend (a Latin scholar) really need to explain to a history scholar that "Sophia" (the main characters "Granna's" name---and don't get me started on <i>that</i> cutesy name) is the Greek for wisdom? Couldn't the author have imparted this information in a less clumsy way? <br /><br />I finally gave up at the end of Chapter 4 in which Connie just happens to meet a handsome fellow scholar while researching a slip of paper in nearby Salem. This scholar just happens to be repairing the steeple of the church so clearly he is good with his hands. Connie has a house that needs a lot of work. Hmmm....Anyone who has watched a Lifetime movie can predict where this story is heading at this point. I might spend a couple of hours giggling with my daughters over this plot if it <i>were</i> a cheesy Lifetime offering (in fact my youngest and I <i>did</i> watch a LT movie about a teenage girl who moves to a spooky old New England house with her mom and is haunted by the ghost of a distant ancestor who was burnt as a witch). I can't justify spending five or six hours on this silly book. <br />I wouldn't even read this at the beach, personally (although I've never been a fan of so-called "beach reads") but that is how I would market this awkward clunker.<br /><br />BTW, I'm surprised at how many reviewers on Goodreads comment on the original plot. I would say it is derivative of <i>Possession</i> for starters. I haven't read <i>The Da Vinci Code</i> or <i>The Historian</i> so I'll have to take other reviewers' word for those influences. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5381810740682688614" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7424597151277624777">I didn't expect this to be good, but neither did I expect it to be THIS BAD. It drives me nuts when people get HUGE book advances and media attention for such sloppy work. What did her editor even DO if she didn't catch the numerous dumb things that don't make sense in this book?   <span id="freeText7424597151277624777" style="display:none">I didn't expect this to be good, but neither did I expect it to be THIS BAD. It drives me nuts when people get HUGE book advances and media attention for such sloppy work. What did her editor even DO if she didn't catch the numerous dumb things that don't make sense in this book? For example, 2/3 of the way through, the heroine has to find a book in Widener library at Harvard WHERE SHE HAS BEING STUDYING FOR THE PAST FOUR YEARS and doesn't know how to find it and has to ask the librarian why it's not in the computer. Um? Also, all the events in the book could have taken place over a long weekend (okay, two weeks max) but for some reason the chapters skip over weeks and months in between, so the action takes more than a summer. Why? No apparent reason. Apart from the oh-so-convenient scenes that allow the heroine to lecture the reader on colonial history that most people learn in grade school, and the cheezy dialogue, the mother is also super frustrating. We're supposed to believe that she knew all the time that people's lives were at stake and still does nothing but hint on the phone, and the one time she gets close to telling Connie something's up, Connie hangs up on her. SO CALL BACK--IT'S JUST A PHONE. I demand much more from my beach reads. Had I actually been at the beach, I would have buried this one in the sand. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_84532480" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/84532480" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2276205-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1285872916p2/2276205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84532480?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/2276205-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401341336" href="/book/show/6987086-the-physick-book-of-deliverance-dane">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84532480" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15379343776464350238">There are a few tropes that will convince me to pick up almost any book that promises to contain them. I’ll call one of them “Searching for a Long-Lost Book,” and another “All My Forebears Were Secretly Witches.” Katherine Howe‘s <i>The Physick Book of Deliverance Dane</i> features both of these, so it’s no surprise that I’d wanted to read it for a long time. I confess I was privately hoping for a book that combined the awesomeness of two of my all-time favorites: A.S. Byatt‘s <i>Possession</i> and Anne Rice‘</span>
  <span id="freeText15379343776464350238" style="display:none">There are a few tropes that will convince me to pick up almost any book that promises to contain them. I’ll call one of them “Searching for a Long-Lost Book,” and another “All My Forebears Were Secretly Witches.” Katherine Howe‘s <i>The Physick Book of Deliverance Dane</i> features both of these, so it’s no surprise that I’d wanted to read it for a long time. I confess I was privately hoping for a book that combined the awesomeness of two of my all-time favorites: A.S. Byatt‘s <i>Possession</i> and Anne Rice‘s <i>The Witching Hour</i>. Despite its inclusion of the Salem witch trials, however, this is a lighter read than either of those: cozier despite the tragic history behind it, and neither as rich nor as twisty.<br /><br />Connie Goodwin is a Harvard graduate student specializing in American colonial history. Just as she’s about to start her dissertation research, her mother asks a favor of her: go through the ancestral house in Marblehead, Massachusetts and clean it up for sale. While exploring the house, Connie learns that a long-ago ancestor, Deliverance Dane, may be a Salem “witch” heretofore lost to history. Unlike the other victims of the witch craze, though, Deliverance may have actually had magical power and left behind a grimoire. Connie is drawn into the mystery of the missing spellbook. The chapters detailing her search are interspersed with flashbacks to the history of Deliverance, her book, and her descendants through the years.<br /><br />Katherine Howe, who is descended from two Salem “witches,” takes us on a vivid tour of Massachusetts. The Physick Book of Deliverance Dane has sense of place in spades, from the old house itself to the sailing bar to the tourist kitsch of modern-day Salem. Another aspect of the setting that should be mentioned is time. The “modern-day” sections take place in 1991, though the book was published in 2009. 1991 is close enough to the present that it’s easy to forget it’s not — that is, until Connie encounters problems that could be solved with a cell phone or the Internet. We wonder why she isn’t making use of these amenities, then realize a moment later that they simply aren’t there.<br /><br />I also enjoyed the character development of Connie. She’s closed-minded and closed-off as the novel begins, and begins to open her life in more ways than one as the story unfolds. There’s an adorable dog, too, which never hurts. Another strong point is Howe’s look at the way women’s writing was often disdained in academia, historically speaking. The way this ties in with the grimoire’s eventual location is clever. And after some of the books I’ve read lately, I have to give Howe props for writing a love interest who isn’t superhuman or creepy or both; he’s just a regular guy, and in fact I liked him better than I did Connie.<br /><br />What doesn’t work is the mystery. First, it relies too heavily on Connie being slow to catch clues that would be unlikely to befuddle a grad student in her field. Second, and more problematically, the villain is just too obvious — painfully so.<br /><br />Don’t read <i>The Physick Book of Deliverance Dane</i> for the mystery aspect. It’s an enjoyable light read if you go in with the right expectations. Expect great atmosphere, troubled mother-daughter relationships, gentle romance, folk magic, and a bit of academic politics, but not much mystery.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15379343776464350238" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_111631833" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/111631833" />
    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2679341-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442635072p2/2679341.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/111631833?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane" href="/user/show/2679341-jane">Jane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401341336" href="/book/show/6987086-the-physick-book-of-deliverance-dane">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2679341-jane?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2679341-jane?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer111631833" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9413027482731547233">Where I got the book: purchased on Amazon. A Sunday in the Park book club read.<br/><br/>The plot: Harvard grad student Connie is assigned by her mother to clear out her grandmother's house, and discovers her connection to the Salem Witches.<br/><br/>I was, on the whole, underwhelmed, which is what happens when you pick up a book that's touted as a Brilliant Bestseller! Spooky! Bedeviling! and find that the writing's that of a newbie writer, good on the important points of grammar and generally stringing words toge</span>
  <span id="freeText9413027482731547233" style="display:none">Where I got the book: purchased on Amazon. A Sunday in the Park book club read.<br /><br />The plot: Harvard grad student Connie is assigned by her mother to clear out her grandmother's house, and discovers her connection to the Salem Witches.<br /><br />I was, on the whole, underwhelmed, which is what happens when you pick up a book that's touted as a Brilliant Bestseller! Spooky! Bedeviling! and find that the writing's that of a newbie writer, good on the important points of grammar and generally stringing words together but rather inconsistent in terms of hanging a plot together believably and generally not annoying the reader. THIS reader was thoroughly annoyed in quite a few places: the old, abandoned house that mysteriously had not had its mains water cut off and was well supplied with oil lamps whose oil had mysteriously not dried up over the last, what, thirty or forty years; the entire function of Sam-the-love-interest who, I swear, was just stuck in the book so he could be In Grave Danger; the slipping into the POV of the dog or even, at one point, a rat; and the absolute worst, the relentless use of New England dialect. Yes, I know that people out there talk funny. I don't need to be reminded of it ALL THE TIME, especially when it's mixed with Ye Olde English to give us the following:<br /><br /><i>"Anothah time," she began again, "I ha' sought physick from heh for a pained foot. She bade me entah heh house and did apply some liniment to my foot which she made by mashing hairbs and readin' in some book. I asked her what book war this and she said nowt but placed the book on a high shelf and asked me if my foot weh feeling bettah, which it was."</i><br /><br />And it really wasn't all that spooky. Or bedeviling. But the stepback cover on this edition was COOL and almost made up for Connie's stupidity. For a Harvard doctoral student she was pretty darn slow on the uptake, missing all the totally obvious clues strewn in her path.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9413027482731547233" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3165163867?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan K" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan K" href="/user/show/32433113-jonathan-k">Jonathan K</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3165163867" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1372345723200953198">Having previously read Katherine's sequel to this book, "The Daughter of Temperance Hobbs", this story filled in the missing blanks, its format identical to the second one. Toggling back and forth between the present and days of Salem witches, the characters interact well, her mother Grace living in NM instead of locally. The reader is introduced to Connie Goodwin as a grad student instead of professor, along with how she 'stumbles' into her grandmother's past. I had hoped for something more pow</span>
  <span id="freeText1372345723200953198" style="display:none">Having previously read Katherine's sequel to this book, "The Daughter of Temperance Hobbs", this story filled in the missing blanks, its format identical to the second one. Toggling back and forth between the present and days of Salem witches, the characters interact well, her mother Grace living in NM instead of locally. The reader is introduced to Connie Goodwin as a grad student instead of professor, along with how she 'stumbles' into her grandmother's past. I had hoped for something more powerful and felt somewhat disappointed. Researching witchcraft and its physick treatments is one thing; building suspense and momentum is another. This is where the story falls flat due to its meandering pace and failure to completely engage. Regardless, it was enjoyable and wraps up any further interest in 'witch stories'. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1372345723200953198" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62288654" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16832274274740306109">Reviewed by Angie Fisher for TeensReadToo.com<br/><br/>Connie Goodwin, like many young women, has issues with her mother. So when Grace asks her daughter to interrupt her doctoral research at Harvard to go to Marblehead for the summer and ready her grandmother's house to be sold, Connie wonders why she agreed.<br/><br/>And that was before she walked into the ancient cottage that has no electricity.<br/><br/>Connie's knowledge of Colonial history, her chosen field of study, comes in handy as she learns the secrets of the past</span>
  <span id="freeText16832274274740306109" style="display:none">Reviewed by Angie Fisher for TeensReadToo.com<br /><br />Connie Goodwin, like many young women, has issues with her mother. So when Grace asks her daughter to interrupt her doctoral research at Harvard to go to Marblehead for the summer and ready her grandmother's house to be sold, Connie wonders why she agreed.<br /><br />And that was before she walked into the ancient cottage that has no electricity.<br /><br />Connie's knowledge of Colonial history, her chosen field of study, comes in handy as she learns the secrets of the past occupants of the house, one of whom was tried in the Salem witch trials. As Connie digs deeper into the past, she finds herself being catapulted into a world of witchery and magic, the very things her own mother has made her run far and fast from.<br /><br />THE PHYSICK BOOK OF DELIVERANCE DANE has it all: believable characters, a love story, friendship, and family connections as well as curses. It is a well-written first novel that weaves the author's personal ties with the era into an incredibly convincing story.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16832274274740306109" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_61792204" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61792204" />
    <a title="Nan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/754607-nan"><img alt="Nan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277820071p2/754607.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61792204?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nan" href="/user/show/754607-nan">Nan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/754607-nan?shelf=borrowed">borrowed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/754607-nan?shelf=university-fiction">university-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61792204" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6017897987494621608">Edit, 7-3-19: on my reread after all these years, I liked the book better. I still have several things that bother me, but I was in a better place for reading the book this time. My original review is behind the spoiler tag, as it contains many spoilers. Overall, it gained one star in my rating.<br/><br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[<br/><br/>I'm glad that I don't own this book. I would have been really annoyed if I bought it in hardcover.<br/><br/>I've clicked the little "spoiler" box here at Goodreads, but if you read this book in anoth</span></span>
  <span id="freeText6017897987494621608" style="display:none">Edit, 7-3-19: on my reread after all these years, I liked the book better. I still have several things that bother me, but I was in a better place for reading the book this time. My original review is behind the spoiler tag, as it contains many spoilers. Overall, it gained one star in my rating.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><br />I'm glad that I don't own this book. I would have been really annoyed if I bought it in hardcover.<br /><br />I've clicked the little "spoiler" box here at Goodreads, but if you read this book in another way, you are hereby warned. This review contains spoilers. Massive spoilers. That's not normally my idea of a good review, but I have to be somewhat specific with my complaint here. There. You're warned. If you keep reading, it's your choice.<br /><br />I found this to be a good, entertaining book on many levels. In the afterword, Katherine Howe noted that there have been several books explaining the reasons behind the Salem Witch Trials. Social anxiety. Conflicts within the church. Greed. Moldy bread. All of these reasons have been covered many times. (The moldy bread is my favorite.) However, she wanted to write a book where the witchcraft was real--as the people of Salem believed that it was real. She acts like this is a new idea. It's not. Celia Rees' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/803120.Witch_Child" title="Witch Child by Celia Rees" rel="nofollow noopener">Witch Child</a> is just of the many other books that works from the same premise.<br /><br />So, lack of original premise aside, how does this book fare? Reasonably well. Howe is a historian, and she incorporates period detail into the novel seamlessly. I don't particularly like her writing style as a whole, however. She was entirely too descriptive--bordering on purple prose. (Not over that violet hued line, but closer than I like.)<br /><br />The first chapter of this book is a peak into Salem's past. The second chapter is Connie's qualifying exam (QE). As a recent Ph.D. candidate myself, I can say that Howe did an excellent job describing the exam. <br /><br />Unfortunately, she also did a great job telegraphing the identity of the villain--as well as every major plot point. There were no surprises here for me. It could be that Howe did a good job foreshadowing those plot points--or it could be that they were just too predictible. I'll leave that for other readers to decide. I don't know whether I should appauld Howe's audicity or groan in pain when in comes to the villain. The greedy, overreaching dissertation director? Too stereotypical to be believed. I think I would have given this novel a higher rating if not for the villain; his identity is pretty clear straight from the QE.<br /><br />Other people may like this book more than I do. In fact, I guarantee that they will--but it should be clear from this review that the book did nothing for me.<br /><br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="6017897987494621608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('eh3o3xasMw2MpSxnhqZcgaTmVkUSEhDxIAExKSP3nluS2X4l/TjxavkBNYQonTxXNaLCefhzmyTt25VZ8XfCvw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tsEH6AUeX905pvhoGRcVJEbYMrG5ov5Nhp3zMONc3KpeBZES7oqdukwC4Yu3LHXy15ymjVPDdZhLR1dAMdyATg==')})
  }

The letters are all addressed to &quot;My dear and unfortunate successor,&quot; and they plunge her into a world she never dreamed of, a labyrinth where the secrets of her father\'s past and her mother\'s mysterious fate connect to an inconceivable evil hidden in the depths of history.\n\nThe letters provide links to one of the darkest powers that humanity has ever known and to a centuries-long quest to find the source of that darkness and wipe it out. It is a quest for the truth about Vlad the Impaler, the medieval ruler whose barbarous reign formed the basis of the legend of Dracula. Generations of historians have risked their reputations, their sanity, and even their lives to learn the truth about Vlad the Impaler and Dracula. Now one young woman must decide whether to take up this quest herself--to follow her father in a hunt that nearly brought him to ruin years ago, when he was a vibrant young scholar and her mother was still alive. What does the legend of Vlad the Impaler have to do with the modern world? Is it possible that the Dracula of myth truly existed and that he has lived on, century after century, pursuing his own unknowable ends? The answers to these questions cross time and borders, as first the father and then the daughter search for clues, from dusty Ivy League libraries to Istanbul, Budapest, and the depths of Eastern Europe. In city after city, in monasteries and archives, in letters and in secret conversations, the horrible truth emerges about Vlad the Impaler\'s dark reign and about a time-defying pact that may have <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13579265801023390933">Katherine Howe is a New York Times bestselling and award-winning writer of historical fiction. Her adult novels are The Physick Book of Deliverance Dane, which debuted at #2 on the New York Times bestseller list in 2009 and was named one of USA Today’s top tend books of the year, and The House of Velvet and Glass, which was a USA Today bestseller in 2011. For young adults, Katherine has written Co</span>
  <span id="freeText13579265801023390933" style="display:none">Katherine Howe is a New York Times bestselling and award-winning writer of historical fiction. Her adult novels are The Physick Book of Deliverance Dane, which debuted at #2 on the New York Times bestseller list in 2009 and was named one of USA Today’s top tend books of the year, and The House of Velvet and Glass, which was a USA Today bestseller in 2011. For young adults, Katherine has written Conversion, which received the 2015 Massachusetts Book Award in young adult literature, and a New York City-based literary ghost story called The Appearance of Annie van Sinderen, which was named a 2016 “Must Read” for young adults by the Massachusetts Center for the Book. In 2014 she edited The Penguin Book of Witches for Penguin Classics, a primary source reader on the history of witchcraft in England and North America which made a regional bestseller list and which has been translated into Spanish. The Daughters of Temperance Hobbs, her new novel for adults, will be published by Henry Holt and Co in summer 2019. She has appeared on “Good Morning America,” “CBS This Morning,” NPR’s “Weekend Edition,” the BBC, and the History Channel, and she hosted “Salem: Unmasking the Devil” for National Geographic. Her fiction has been translated into over twenty languages. In 2015 she was the visiting writer in residence at Lenoir-Rhyne University in North Carolina, and she spent 2016 as a visiting scholar at the Center for Advanced Study in the Behavioral Sciences at Stanford. She holds a BA in art history and philosophy from Columbia and an MA in American and New England studies from Boston University, and she has taught American history, visual culture, and writing at BU and Cornell. Her first published works were three brief catalogue essays in an architectural monograph published by the Museum of Modern Art in 2000. A native Houstonian, she lives in New England and New York City with her family, where she is at work on her next novel. She also puts hot sauce on everything.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13579265801023390933" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
